ii

ABSTRACT SENSITIVITY OF RODEL ROUNDABOUT DELAY ESTIMATES TO INPUT PARAMETERS
Nasim Norouzi Master of Applied Science in Civil Engineering (2009) Department of Civil Engineering Ryerson University, Toronto Modern roundabouts are developed to increase the traffic capacity and decrease the traffic delay. They are popular around the world and are becoming common in Canada and the United States. A great design of a roundabout is to achieve the minimum delay. According to the research papers regarding the delay at roundabouts, it is expected the vehicle delay, the stop ratio at various traffic volumes, and the level of saturation of roundabouts are smaller than the amount of similar parameters on un-signalized intersections. The purpose of this thesis is clarification and measurement of the geometric parameters through the RODEL software to depict their influence on the sensitivity of roundabout delay. This study has been done through creation and analysis of numerous tables and graphs for various traffic volumes and peak periods. Furthermore, the required accuracy of each parameter in estimating delay of a planned roundabout is determined.

The outcome of this analysis indicates the effects of the geometric parameters on the roundabout delay. While the Half Width, Entry Width, and Flare Length have the greatest influence on the delay, the Entry Radius, Entry Angle (PHI), and Inscribed Circle Diameter have the least influence.

iii

CHAPTER ONE: INTRODUCTION 1.1 Research Background
Transportation plays a significant role in everybody's life. People travel to different destinations for various purposes. The main reasons for travelling are categorized into the personal, social, business, or economical basis. People use different transportation facilities depending on their time and other constraints, as well as the related costs. The main form of daily transportation is by motor vehicles because these provide quick access to destinations, convenient use of private or public vehicles, and are affordable to most people. However, there are some disadvantages involved in use of motor vehicles including traffic congestion and delay, air pollution, noise nuisance, and land consumption, especially in large and crowd cities. Working on reduction of the delay time has been one of the greatest attempts to resolve the disadvantages of travelling by motor vehicles in Canada and the United States during the past decade.

Since the time automobile was invented, the struggle to control and manage the traffic has been in place. In the early 1900's, the traffic crisis was most prevalent in the United States and Europe. In that era, traffic circles were considered as a useful treatment to resolve the traffic problem. However, in 1950's, the traffic circles with the size and other characteristics of that time did not represent a permanent solution to the traffic congestion because of increasing the traffic volume and the delay time at rotator junctions (Flannery and Datta, 1996). The old techniques of designing the traffic circle intersections improved in 1990's and converted to a vital solution to control traffic and minimize the delay time. 1

Delay minimization is an essential goal of transportation planning to improve the performance of planning models. Delay ignorance may cause an inaccurate value predictions and incorrect estimation of the travel time. Since the delay time may have direct influence on the level of service or loss of productivity, it could significantly impact on economic implications as well. The requirements to run a planning model and improve the accuracy of travel time estimation are geometry information of the assigned roundabout and its traffic volume (Ding, 2007).

1.2 Scope and Objectives
Generally, roundabouts are one of the best remedies to resolve the traffic flow problems. These problems include how to deal with conflicting manoeuvres and speed reduction in sensitive areas. Slow or congested traffic would be the result of poor performance of an improper roundabout. Therefore, understanding the behaviour of roundabouts in different demand patterns is essential in order to prevent using them in inappropriate conditions. An example of inappropriate conditions is high traffic volume on one approach that may affect the traffic of the entry vehicles of the other approaches. In this case, the roundabout responds to the total traffic volume fairly reasonable but excessive delays would appear on some approaches due to the unbalanced traffic volume (Krogscheepers and Roebuck, 1997).

As it was mentioned earlier, roundabouts have become a subject of interest in American and Canadian transportation societies over the past decade. There are various situations for implementation of roundabouts throughout the United States and Canada. In many states, roundabouts are considered as a practical alternative to two-way stop controlled intersections (TWSC), all-way stop controlled intersections (AWSC), and in some cases for signalized and 2

complex freeway interchanges. Since roundabouts are becoming more popular than the above noted intersections in the United States, existence of a complete guideline regarding the design, construction, operation and maintenance, and safety of the roundabouts is necessary. There are a few guidelines such as the Federal Highway Administration (FHWA) and the Highway Capacity Manual (HCM) available in the United States (Flannery, et. al., 2000). The Ministry of Transportation of Ontario (MTO) is willing to create a complete and applicable guideline regarding the modern roundabouts. This guideline is expected to highlight the importance and sensitivity of the delay time in details and how to manage this parameter through the best design and operation of the roundabouts.

The objective of this study is to evaluate the sensitivity of the geometric parameters of roundabouts in respect with the minimal delay time at various traffic volumes and different timeframes. Also, this thesis aims to reveal the required accuracy for each parameter.

The analyzed traffic volume includes the Average Annual Daily Traffic (AADT) of three existing roundabouts within the Region of Waterloo, as well as the AADT of 2000, 3000, and 4000 vehicles. The time-frames of morning, noon, and after noon were taken into account to identify the impact of traffic peak hours in the study.

1.3 Thesis Structure
This thesis includes nine chapters. Chapter 1 offers an introduction, importance of a roundabout, and the scope and objective of this study. Chapter 2 is devoted to the birth of modern roundabout, its characteristics, advantages and disadvantages compared to traditional 3

intersections, and the traffic flow problems. Chapter 3 is dedicated to the research papers regarding the delays associated with intersections and roundabouts. Chapter 4 is expressly focused on roundabouts' capacity and delays which is the main theme of this study. Chapter 5 is about the roundabout operation and related analysis tools/models. Chapter 6 introduces the RODEL characteristics as a software program for design and operation of a roundabout with minimal delay. Chapter 7 presents the analysis methodology of this thesis and outlines the data related to three existing roundabouts that have been used in this study. Chapter 8 is solely dedicated to the analysis and the achieved results with respect to the sensitivity of each geometric parameter and the related delay time in different traffic volumes and time-frames. Chapter 9 is the thesis closure, which includes the conclusions and recommendations for further improvements in design and operation of modern roundabouts.

4

CHAPTER TWO: MODERN ROUNDABOUTS 2.1 Birth of Modern Roundabouts
Old traffic circles called roundabouts have existed as long as cars. The history of roundabouts goes back to the 19th century and their actual design is originated from large traffic circles in France. Eugène Hénard brought up the attitude of an intersection in Paris where all the road users, mainly horses and coaches, had to make a circulatory movement around an obstacle in the center in 1903. Afterward, more roundabouts were built in Europe, especially in Great Britain. Roundabouts became common in Europe during the 1980's and 1990's (Daniels and Wets, 2005). Figures 2.1 and 2.2 show plan and picture, respectively, of the Triumph Arc roundabout in Paris, France. There are 12 approaching streets that flow the traffic into the circle.

Figure 2.1 Triumph Arc Roudabout Plan, Paris, France (GoogleMaps, 2009)

5

Figure 2.2 Triumph Arc Roundabout Picture, Paris, France (GoogleImages, 2009)

The first one-way rotary system (roundabout) for motor vehicle traffic in the United States was put into operation at Columbus Circle in New York City in 1905 (Persaud, et. al., 2000). Subsequently, other roundabouts were added to the United States transportation system. Previously, the roundabouts design criteria gave the priority to the high speed merging and weaving vehicles. Thus, congestion and high crash experiences occurred in the traffic circles and caused loss of confidence and finally led to roundabout failure in the United States after the mid 1950's. For example, traffic circles in New Jersey were removed after recieving complains about their poor performance and high level of crashes that occurred because of long delay (Sisiopiku and Oh, 2001). The experience of utilizing traffic circles in the transportation system of many countries, in addition to the United States, failed due to increasing traffic volume and high crashes. Consequently, many of those traffic circles were replaced by traffic signals (Robinson, et al., 2000).

Modern roundabouts were borne, as a remedy to the problems associated with the old traffic circles, in the United Kingdom (U.K.) in 1966. There was a mandatory rule called "Give6

Way" applied at all circular intersections in U.K. This rule makes entering traffic to give priority or yield to the circulating traffic. The main application of the "Give-Way" rule was to prevent locking up the circular intersection by not letting vehicles enter the intersection until they find enough gaps to enter (MDOT, 2009). Reducing traffic congestion, and enhancing roundabout capacity and safety are the result of applying this rule at modern roundabouts (Taekratok, 1998).

The performance of modern roundabouts in other countries were motivating enough to generate a recent version of roundabout in the United States and Canada (Sisiopiku and Oh, 2001). Modern roundabouts have become significantly popular in these countries over the past decade. It is one of the best remedies to overcome the intersection problems (Gates and Maki, 2002). Figure 2.3 reflects the development process of modern roundabouts between the years 1900 to 2000.

Figure 2.3 Development Process of Modern Roundabout between 1900-2000 (Taekratok, 1998)

The next section consists of description of modern roundabout characteristics and its advantages against signalized and un-signalized intersections.

7

2.2 Modern Roundabout Characteristics
As it was mentioned, the modern roundabouts were originally developed in the United Kingdom in 1960's and nowadays they are widely used all around the world. One of the main differences of the modern roundabouts and old traffic circles is the design speed. According to the guidelines of 1940s or even older, the design speed for old traffic circles was 25 mph or more. This speed for the modern roundabouts is considered about 15 mph to provide a higher level of safety. In this respect, the motorists entering a roundabout have to adjust their speed with a deflection curve to slow down the travel speed and keep weaving to a minimum (Persaud, et. al., 2000). The reason for installation of signal or stop signs at old traffic circles was to reduce the number of crashes due to the high travel speed. The size characteristic of the modern roundabouts causes a lower travel speed for entry or circulating vehicles and removes the need to utilize signal or stop signs. Drivers approaching a roundabout must reduce their speed, look for a safe gap within the circulating traffic flow, and always be ready to stop for crossing pedestrians and bicycles. Movement of vehicles in modern roundabouts especially in the United States and Canada is counter-clockwise around the central island. Modern roundabout can be defined as a circular intersection with characteristics that promote safe and efficient traffic flow (IIHS, 2009). Figure 2.4 shows a large old traffic circle that has been transformed to a modern roundabout.

8

Figure 2.4 Old traffic circle transformation to modern roundabout (Ourston, 2009) Yield-at-entry, deflection, and flare are three other features that distinguish roundabouts from traffic circles. These features are defined below and the designer should be aware of them as three major characteristics of the modern roundabouts (Hicks, 1996). 1. Yield-at-entry allows circulating flow vehicles to continue moving through a roundabout towards their appointed exits and eliminates congestion that may occur while right-of-way is given to the entering (Hicks, 1996). Yield-at-entry can increase the capacity and decrease the delay of roundabouts by 10% and 40% respectively (Sisiopiku and Oh, 2001).

Figure 2.5 The Yield sign at Roundabout (GoogleImage, 2009) 9

2. Deflection is defined by the curve around the central island that causes drivers to slow down through the roundabout. This factor increases the safety by reducing the circulating speed (Hicks, 1996).

Figure 2.6 Deflection in roundabout (GoogleImage, 2009)

3.

Flare represents widening of the approach in order to increase the capacity (Hicks, 1996).

Figure 2.7 Flare in roundabout (GoogleImage, 2009) 10

11

The main advantages of replacing the traditional intersections and nonconforming traffic circles with modern roundabouts are enhancing the safety and capacity. Speed reduction while approaching, entering, and moving in a roundabout because of the deflected entry is a good example of higher safety exertion through roundabouts. Furthermore, yielding at the roundabout entry provides a higher capacity than the traditional intersections. This higher capacity in turn, causes less delay to occur (IIHS, 2009). Therefore, Modern roundabout can be a great alternative to resolve the intersection problems. The best performance of a roundabout can be achieved in the following circumstances (Niederhauser, et. al., 1997):  Location which is the area with some/all of the following descriptions:  High accident history or heavy left turn movements;  Capacity or noticeable delay problem at intersection;  Intersections in which traffic signals were requested but not warranted;  Intersection that meets warrant for traffic signal; and  Four way stop intersection.  Traffic volume with:  Significant delay on minor road;  Heavy left turn movements that cause traffic signals less efficient.  Right of way consideration:  Generally desired more for single-lane roundabout than an un-signalized intersection;

12

 Roundabout usually needs less entry lanes in comparison with signal controlled intersection.  Site conditions:  An intersection with more than four approaches or unusual geometry;  Major roads intersect at "Y" or "T" junctions;  Intersection with anticipation of high growth of future traffic;  Intersection in which the U-turn is desirable;

2.4 Modern Roundabout as a Solution for the Traffic Flow Problems
The major traffic flow problems are high delay, long queue length, and high left-turn volume. These problems can be resolved through replacement of the problematic intersections with roundabouts.

2.4.1 High Delay Intersections
Roundabout is known as a good alternative to signalized or un-signalized intersections. The reason is that in signalized intersections, a certain stream of vehicles is legally allowed to proceed through at a time. In un-signalized intersections, the vehicles have to stop at stop signs as well. These could cause a high delay and capacity loss when the intersection is clear between signal phases and stops respectively. Utilizing roundabouts eliminates this problem because the entry vehicles can use the available gaps within the circulating flow and reduce the delay time. Therefore, the modern roundabouts are more efficient than controlled intersections (KYTC, 2005).

13

2.4.2 Long Queue Lengths
Widened roads are usually required to provide the necessary storage area for vehicles waiting at traffic signals. Roundabouts allow the use of fewer lanes between intersections because continuous flows produce less delay. This improvement reduces the queue lengths and minimizes the operational problems at downstream intersections or where queuing space is limited (KYTC, 2005).

2.4.3 High Left-Turn Volume Intersections
In signalized intersections, separate turning phases may be required to accommodate leftturning vehicles. When a high volume of left-turning vehicles is anticipated, those phases must be increased. In this case, a longer cycle length to complete the signal phases is required. Therefore, more delay to the movement on other lanes occurs. A roundabout may accommodate left-turning vehicles more efficiently due to its characteristic in terms of lower delay (KYTC, 2005).

14

CHAPTER THREE: RONDABOUT DELAY PROCESSING
The consequence of increasing the traffic congestion in large urban areas is deterioration of the traffic management. However, in many cases the transportation planning models, which estimate the appropriate traffic volume on transportation networks, are incapable of considering the realistic travel time delays at intersections. Considering the effect of delays at intersections and roundabouts are important to present the model output more accurate and the travel time more predictable (Ding, 2007). There are many techniques for delay estimation at intersection approaches. However, limited researches have been done to assess the consistency of various models estimations. Incapability of delay estimation models is based on their type of data requirements and algorithms. The problem with the delay estimation techniques is that they have their own limitations and come up with different accuracies. For instance when the ratio of volume over capacity (V/C) approaches to 1.0, steady-state models tend to estimate large delays while oversaturation models yield delays close to zero. Therefore, making a balance between simplicity and accuracy is essential in choosing the right modeling technique (Ding, 2007). Control delay and queue delay are two main components of determining the intersection delays. Control delay occurs due to the vehicles acceleration or deceleration at intersection because of the traffic control. Queue delay or stop delay is difficult to estimate because it is affected by random arrival of vehicles from different approaches (Ding, 2007). Queue and traffic delays are principals for the performance measures in order to determine the Level of Service (LOS) of an intersection. These performance measures are based on assumptions about the description of the traffic arrival and service processes. Therefore, the

15

delay models at intersections are defined according to deterministic and stochastic components to illustrate the traffic flow properties (Rouphail, et. al., 1996).

3.1 Deterministic and Stochastic Components of Delay
The deterministic (often called uniform) component of traffic delay is defined by fluid theory when demand and service are treated as continuous variables. These variables are expressed by traffic flow rates which differ with the time and locality. The deterministic queuing models are traditional applications to foresee delays at signalized intersections (Rouphail, et. al., 1996). The stochastic (random or overflow) components of delay are defined on steady-state queuing theory. This theory describes the traffic arrival time and service time distribution. Then, proper queuing model would be applied to this distribution in order to analyze the performance measures. The stochastic approach is mainly used for un-signalized intersections. However, the models which include both deterministic and stochastic components of traffic performance are more desirable in traffic signals because they are very applicable in a wide range of traffic intensities. The pioneer work in this respect started by Webster in 1958 and since then, it has been incorporated in control and analysis of many intersections around the world (Rouphail, et. al., 1996). Generally, there are two methods of delay estimation at intersections based on the steadystate and time-dependent theories. Webster and HCM 2000 methods are the examples of the delay estimation with steady-state theory, whereas, Akcelik and Kimber and Hollis methods employ time-dependent theory (Sermpis, 2007). The following sections describe some of the researches regarding delay estimation. 16

3.1.1 Webster Model
Webster operated a series of experiments on pre-time isolated intersection in 1958. This model is based on the number of arrivals in a certain time interval and follows a known distribution, which does not change over the time. This model employs two traffic signal timing strategies: signal phase splits and minimum delay cycle length equation (Cheng, et. al., 2003). Webster could illustrate signal phase both theoretically and experimentally. According to his study, signals should have their critical phases timed for equal degree of saturation for a given cycle length in order to minimize the delay. Equation 3.1 shows the minimum delay cycle length (Cheng, et. al., 2003): 0 =

This formula is a clear example of a model that corresponds to the both deterministic and stochastic components of the traffic performance. "The Webster's formula is one of the most fundamental and frequently referenced equations of its kind" (Ding, 2007).

3.1.2 Tanner Model
Tanner studied the delay on minor stream based on the gap acceptance model in 1962 (Sisiopiku and Oh, 2001). He considered the following assumptions in his study (Tanner, 1962):  1: vehicles on major road of an intersection arrive randomly and pass through within not less than 1 time;  2: vehicles on minor road of an intersection arrive randomly and pass through within not less than 2 time;  : duration of time (gap) that a vehicle cannot enter the junction after the previous vehicle on the major road has entered.

18

According to Tanner's study, the traffic on the major road of an intersection consists of a random and single stream of arriving vehicles which cannot pass through the junction at intervals shorter than the time 1. Thus, the vehicles on the major road form a queuing process. In this situation, the average delay is calculated using the Equation 3.3 which is known as the Tanner formula (Tanner, 1962).
2 1 (1 q1 ) 2

(1 - 1 q1 )

Equation 3.3

Where, w1 is the average delay per vehicle; q1 is an average rate per time unit for major road; q2 is an average rate per time unit for minor road. Vehicles on the minor road of an intersection behave similarly. Their arrival traffic is randomly and at an average rate of q2 per time unit. This traffic cannot pass through the junction at intervals shorter than 2. A vehicle on minor road can pass through the major road traffic in the gap time between ( and  + 2). However, in major road traffic, two vehicles can pass through a gap time of ( + 2) and ( + 22) and so on (Tanner, 1962).

3.1.3 Kimber and Hollis Model
Kimber and Hollis method, which was based on the time-dependent delay model, developed in 1979 to improve intersection delay. An appropriate transformation technique was implemented to attain an approximate empirical solution to the problem of oversaturated conditions (Pretorius and Troutbeck, 2004). 19

When traffic flow approaches or exceeds the capacity rate for a limited time, the steadystate model assumptions violate one another because a state of stochastic equilibrium cannot be acquired. Therefore, the time-dependent approach can be used to improve the traffic performance estimation in both under-saturated and over-saturated situations. Kimber and Hollis introduced a procedure to calculate the expected queue length for a system with random arrivals. The initial queue could be defined by its distribution. This method has been adopted by many countries including U.S., Canada, Australia, and Europe and used in their capacity guidelines (Rouphail, et. al., 1996). "Kimber and Hollis gave detailed derivations of queue length and delay formulas, and the resulting formula can be applied to treat not only signalized intersections, but also roundabout. The random and oversaturation delay can be estimated from Equation 3.4" (Han, 1996). r =

20

The Kimber and Hollis model did not address the accuracy issues of their formulas completely. Their concern was only probabilities of fixed queue lengths and they did not consider the mean of both queue lengths and waiting times. They used briefed formulas to calculate the probabilities which may lead to significant errors in the mean of queue lengths (Taylor, 2002). Since the Kimber and Hollis model was based on simplified variables, they suggested using it as an equation to determine the delay of minor traffic (Sisiopiku and Oh, 2001).

3.1.4 Crown Model (RODEL)
Barry Crown developed a program called RODEL (Roundabout Delay) in 1988. This software was created with specific attention to the features that affect on optimization of the roundabout design. The RODEL characteristics can be summarized in the following points (Johnson, 2007):  Achieving solutions for even difficult design situations;  Quickly test "what-if" scenarios by changing the input parameters;  Optimizing the design in a short period of time rather than accepting the first design with meeting the minimum criteria;  Capability to run in different confidence levels to improve the safety factor of traffic predictions;  Derive the optimum layout within the conflicting constraints of delay and safety;  Reduce land and service cost.

21

3.1.5 Akcelik Model (SIDRA)
Akcelik's study was regarding Signalized and Un-signalized Intersections Design and Research Aid (SIDRA). The purpose of using this Australian software was analyzing roundabout capacity and performance. SIDRA was "an extension of traditional gap acceptance and queuing theory" (Sisiopiku and Oh, 2001). According to Akcelik (1997), there are two situations for roundabout capacity, which are related to the gap-acceptance theory. The blocked period's situation happens when the approaching vehicles cannot find an acceptable gap in the circulating flow. The unblocked periods take place when queued or un-queued vehicles can get into the circulation road as soon as gap becomes available in the circulating flow. Blocked and unblocked periods work similar to the red and green signal timing. The total of the blocked and unblocked times called gap acceptance cycle time. Therefore, the gap acceptance capacity in roundabout can be stated similar to traffic signal capacity (Solomon, 2007). According to Akcelik in 1998, the most important factor that affects on roundabout capacity and performance was an unequal lane operation. In this respect, lane by lane analysis was a model to analyze the roundabout capacity. Application of this model was for travel forecasting and determining lane operation through balancing the delay on the approach lanes. This model provides the opportunity for each vehicle to choose the approach lanes which allow entering the roundabout with the least delay (Solomon, 2007).

3.1.6 Highway Capacity Model (HCM)
Roundabout capacity analysis is based on the gap acceptance theory in the HCM 2000. "Gap-acceptance models can adequately predict roundabout capacity within certain ranges of 22

traffic flows. However, outside these ranges gap-acceptance models can over-predict capacity at low traffic flows and under-predict capacity at high traffic flows. At low flows, vehicles often react to enter a major traffic stream more slowly" (Ourston, 2009). The inadequacy of this model is that there are no calculations for control delay; also the analysis is limited to the roundabouts with only one-lane (Stanek and Milam, 2004). The single-lane roundabouts are similar to the TWSC intersections and have the priority and non-priority traffic streams. A driver on a minor approach must check the major traffic stream to find an acceptable gap before entering the priority traffic stream. Gap acceptance theory has three features regarding traffic streams (Flannery, et. al., 2000):  Headway distribution, which is the availability of gap in conflicting or circulating stream;  Gap requirements for entering drivers;  Follow-up time, which is duration of time in which the drivers follow each other into larger gaps. The gap acceptance model has been adopted by many countries such as the United States, Canada, and Australia (Flannery, et. al., 2000).

23

CHAPTER FOUR: ROUNDABOUT CAPACITY AND DELAY
In recent decades, roundabouts have been proved as a viable intersection treatment on both urban and rural roadways. Design of roundabouts is a precise procedure with consideration of capacity, safety, and cost aspects. These principles provide engineers the flexibility to modify a design and meet the related requirements (Furtado, 2004). According to National Cooperative Highway Research Program (NCHRP) 3-65, when roundabouts operate at their capacity, there are usually lower vehicle delays than as it occurs at the other types of intersections. Capacity analysis is based on gap acceptance theory and/or empirical relationships among the geometric parameters of a roundabout (Rodegerdts, 2004).

4.1 Roundabout Capacity
The capacity of every entry to a roundabout is defined as the maximum number of vehicles that can enter into the roundabout in a certain period of time and under the existing traffic and roundabout's geometric conditions. An operational analysis establishes a specific set of geometric parameters and traffic flow rate for the design hour volume (DHV) of each entry. Also, peak hour analysis is a critical appraisal to assess the performance level of the roundabout and its individual components. The yield point of a roundabout is directly related to the capacity analysis because the approach capacity is determined at the yield point (WisDOT, 2008). The capacity of a roundabout as a whole depends on different features. These features include the capacity of each approach or entry (computed as a traffic function on the other approaches), the interaction of the traffic streams, and geometry of the intersection (WisDOT, 2008).

24

The maximum flow rate at a roundabout entry depends on two conditions: the circulating flow and the geometric elements. Lower circulating flow allows driver to enter the roundabout without causing any significant delay. Larger gaps in the circulating flow let more than one vehicle to enter the roundabout. By increasing the circulating flow, the size of the gaps in the circulating flow decreases. Consequently, the number of vehicles entering the roundabout would decrease, as well (WisDOT, 2008). Regarding the geometric elements, the ones with highest effect on determination of the capacity are the entry width, number of lanes at each approach, the flare length, and the width of the circulatory roadway. The other elements, the inscribed circle diameter and the entry angle (phi), have minor effect on capacity (WisDOT, 2008).

4.2 Capacity Analysis
There are three factors to determine the capacity performance of a roundabout: degree of saturation, delay, and queue length (Robinson and Rodegerdts, 2001).

4.2.1 Degree of Saturation
The ratio of the demand at the roundabout entry over the entry capacity is described as the degree of saturation. Although there is not any source to specify an exact amount for the degree of saturation for an entry lane, there are several sources recommending it less than 0.85 in order to have a satisfactory operation. Any amount of degree of saturation over 0.85 could cause the roundabout operation get worse in a short period of time because queue may form and delay begins to increase (Robinson and Rodegerdts, 2001).

25

4.2.2 Delay
Delay is a standard parameter to find out the performance of an intersection. According to the Highway Capacity Manual, delay is known as the principal measure of efficiency for all types of intersections through determinining the level of service (Robinson and Rodegerdts, 2001).

4.2.3 Queue Length
Queue length is an indicator that reflects the proper design of the geometric parameters for a roundabout approaches. Equation 4.1 represents the procedure of calculating an approaximate 95th-percentile queue. The equation is valid when the volume to capaity ratio right before and after the study period is not greater than 0.85 (Robinson and Rodegerdts, 2001).

26

4.3 Roundabout Delay 4.3.1 Control Delay
Control delay is a period of time a driver spends in queuing situation and waits for an acceptable gap in the circulating flow while at the front of the queue. The formula to calculate control delay calculation is illustrated in the Equation 4.2 (Robinson and Rodegerdts, 2001):

 =

Equation 4.2

Where, D is the average control delay (sec/veh); vx is the flow rate for movement, x (veh/hr); cm,x is the capacity of movement, x (veh/hr); T is for the analysis time period, (hr., T=0.25 for a 15-minute period). Control delay increases exponentially based on the volumes while approaching capacity. Therefore, any small change in the volume causes a significant impact on delay. In order to achieve an accurate delay analysis under conditions close or greater than saturation, the following critical factors should be taken into account (Robinson and Rodegerdts, 2001):  The effect of residual queues: when roundabout entries are operated near or over capacity situation, significant residual queues can be created. These residual queues must be considered between consecutive time periods.

27

 The metering effect of upstream oversaturated entries: when an entry located at upstream of the concerned entry is being operated over capacity, the circulating volume at the downstream entry will not flow properly and as per demand. Consequently, the capacity of the downstream entry should be considered more than what it is found through the actual demand analysis.

Average control delay in a particular lane is related to the capacity of that lane and its degree of saturation. As it is reflected in Equation 4.2, the control delay is estimated based on the assumption of the demand less than capacity for the period of analysis. The length of the analysis period has a significant effect on the average control delay when the degree of saturation is greater than about 0.9. In most cases, the recommended analysis period is 15 minutes. If demand gets over capacity during a 15-min period, the calculated control delay may not be accurate. Furthermore, the over-capacity flow conflicts with the downstream entry movements in over-saturated situations (Robinson, et. al., 2005).

4.3.2 Delay Components
Delay components in roundabouts are known in two categories: queuing delay and geometric delay (Hicks, 1996).

4.3.2.1 Queuing delay
Queuing delay is defined as the drivers' waiting time to find an acceptable gap and enter into circulating traffic. In order to calculate the queuing delay, it is important to know the minimum delay when the entering traffic flow is very low. Equations 4.3 and 4.4 show the minimum delay and queuing delay respectively (Hicks, 1996).

The Equation 4.4 is a time-dependent formula which is derived from the steady-state conditions. The second term of this equation reflects presence of a queue in the entry lane. It is a suitable formula to use at near-capacity and over-saturated conditions (Flannery, et. al., 2000). 29

4.3.2.2 Geometric delay
Geometric delay could be described in two ways (Hicks, 1996): 1) Geometric delay is the delay that requires drivers to reduce their speed, enter into the roundabout, and then accelerate to match up the normal speed. 2) Geometric delay is the delay that makes drivers to slow down and stop at the end of the queue, accept a gap, start moving again and enter the roundabout, and then accelerate to reach normal operating speed. The average geometric delay can be computed through the Equation 4.5 (Hicks, 1996):  =   + 1 -   Where, ps is the proportion of the entering vehicles that must stop; ds is the geometric delay for the vehicles that must stop; (1-ps) is the proportion of vehicles that do not need to stop; du is the geometric delay for the vehicles that do not need to stop. "Geometric delay is different for each traffic movement; left-turn, right-turn, and straight on at each approach and each should be calculated separately" (Hicks, 1996). Total average delay is the sum of the queuing delay and the geometric delay. The total average delay per vehicle from an approach must be estimated based on the related delay for proportion of vehicles making each movement (right-turn, straight-on, left-turn) (Hicks, 1996). Equation 4.5

30

4.4 Roundabout Capacity Analysis
In order to verify the capacity, knowing the delay and queuing models is necessary. The HCM method, as a gap acceptance model, is limited to analysis of only single-lane roundabouts and does not present any guidance on delay, queuing, or level of service. The FHWA method, which covers both single-lane and double-lane roundabouts, is derived from the UK empirical model. The UK empirical model, which assumes values for the six geometric input parameters, appears to be a reasonable design tool (Rodegerdts, 2004). UK model calculates the delay during operation at or near capacity in addition to measurement of the capacity itself. Other theories, mentioned in the Chapter Three, provide satisfactory results when operation is either well below capacity or well above it. Research works has shown that when traffic loads increase through capacity, practical/real estimation of the capacity could be achieved. This estimation is based on the actual build up of queues and delays by developing a transformation that gradually moves delay from the stage of the steady state theory (i.e. good at low demand levels) to the stage of the deterministic theory (i.e. accurate when demand is well above capacity) (KSDOT, 2001). The ratio of volume to capacity (V/C) and the level of service (LOS) are the evaluation factors for roundabouts. The V/C ratio defines comparison of the entering traffic volume from an approach to its capacity. Therefore, by increasing the circulating roadway traffic, the capacity of an approach would decrease. Traffic engineers consider a reserve capacity for an approach to correspond this case. The V/C ratio over 0.85 reflects a potential need for additional capacity on an approach. Consideration of too much reserved capacity may result designing an unsafe roundabout. Thus, keeping a balance between the reserved capacity and geometry of a roundabout is important to have a safe and precise operational capacity (Ritchie, 2006). 31

CHAPTER FIVE: ROUNDABOUT OPERATION
A roundabout is perfectly designed when its operational efficiency is at highest level possible. Average delay in a roundabout is an index to verify the operational efficiency. A minor change in geometry/design of a roundabout may result in noticeable changes in its operation (Mehmood and Easa, 2006). Since utilizing roundabouts is becoming more popular in the United States and Canada, investigation of how to determine their level of operational performance is important.

5.1 Traffic Operation
A roundabout minimizes conflicting traffic streams because it allows the streams to safely enter and pass through the roundabout, and exit to the desired directions. The bases of traffic operation in a roundabout are the driver behaviour and the traffic capacity. The geometric elements of a roundabout could influence the driver behaviour and lead them while approaching, entering, and traveling through the roundabout (Robinson, et. al., 2000). The width of the approach roadway, the entry radius, and the approaching traffic volume govern the driver's speed. As drivers approach the yield line, they must check for conflicting vehicles already on the circulating roadway and recognize when it is the safest time to enter the circulating stream. The inscribed circle diameter affects the radius of the driver's path. This geometry element determines the speed of drivers traveling on the roundabout. The width of the circulatory roadway reflects the number of vehicles that may travel side by side on the roundabout (Robinson, et. al., 2000). The capacity of a properly designed roundabout is provided at the yield line. This is determined by a number of geometric parameters. The entry width, half width, and flare length

32

have the most effect on the capacity analysis. Reduction of these elements would decrease the capacity. On multi-lane roundabouts it is important to balance the traffic volume. In imbalance situation, some lanes may be overloaded while others are under-used (Robinson, et. al., 2000). Downsizing the geometry of a roundabout causes lowering the speed of the entry and circulating traffic, which in turn provides a higher level of safety. On the other hand, the capacity of roundabout is negatively affected when the traffic speed is lowered. Hence, a great design of a roundabout is a matter of optimal balancing of safety provisions and operational performance (Robinson, et. al., 2000).

5.2 Existing Roundabout Operation (Field Observation)
As discussed in the previous section, operational efficiency and safety of a roundabout could be optimized through designing it with proper geometric elements. However, evaluation of the existing roundabouts regarding their safety and performance efficiency is another necessity to ensure that an appropriate geometry is in place. The analyst should investigate and determine whether changes to an existing roundabout design are needed. Vehicle delay and queuing can be measured through standard traffic engineering techniques. Furthermore, the analyst is required to perform a qualitative assessment of the roundabout performance. In this respect, the following questionnaire reflects the conditions that may lead to implement corrective design measures. If the answers are negative, no corrective actions are required (Robinson, et. al., 2000).  Do drivers stop unnecessarily at the yield point?  Do drivers stop unnecessarily within the circulating roadway?  Do any vehicles pass on the wrong direction around the central island?  Do queues from an external blockage back up into the roundabout from an exit road? 33

 Do smaller vehicles encroach on the truck apron?  Is there evidence of damage to any of the signs in the roundabout?  Is there any pedestrian activity on the central island?  Do pedestrians and cyclists fail to use the roundabout as intended?  Are there tire marks on any of the curb surfaces to indicate vehicle contact?  Is there any evidence of minor accidents, such as broken glass, pieces of rim, etc., on the approaches or the circulating roadway?  Is there any gravel or other debris collected in non-traveled areas that could be a hazard to bicycles or motorcyclists?  Are the vehicle speeds appropriate?

5.3 Roundabout Operational Analysis Tools
There are two major methods to examine the operational performance of roundabouts: empirical regression technique and gap acceptance method. The empirical regression technique deals with the geometric features which impact driver behaviour. The gap acceptance method is based on the idea that when an individual driver, at a controlled intersection, intends to enter or cross an uncontrolled traffic stream, should wait for an acceptable gap. In case of a roundabout, the entering driver must yield to circulating traffic. Once a driver approaches the yield bar, he/she begins to scan the traffic to his/her left and enters into the circulating traffic when finds an acceptable gap (Flannery and Datta, 1996). There is particular software programs used to design and analyze roundabout capabilities based on various theoretical methods and input parameters. The main purpose of using any

34

related software is to determine the sensitivity of roundabout operational parameters (Kinzel and Trueblood, 2004). The basis of these software programs are analytical or empirical models. The Highway Capacity (HCS), Synchro and SIDRA are analytical software, which are designed based on the gap acceptance theory. RODEL and ARCADY are the empirical software, which are based on the field data and develop relationship between geometric features and performance measures including delay and capacity (Kinzel and Trueblood, 2004) (Fleming, 2008).

5.3.1 Analytical Models 5.3.1.1 HCS/Synchro
"The methodology of Highway Capacity Manual, implemented in the HCS and Synchro programs, can be considered the "official" analysis method of the United States practitioners. It uses the following two parameters to compute roundabout operations in addition to the circulating conflicting volume" (Kinzel and Trueblood, 2004):



Critical Gap (tc): the minimum time interval in the circulating traffic stream that

allows one vehicle to enter the roundabout. A driver would enter the roundabout when faced with any gap equal or greater than the critical gap. Therefore, increasing the critical gap would decrease approach capacity;  Follow-Up Time (tf): the time between the entries of two vehicles into the

roundabout using the same gap in circulating traffic under a condition of continuous

35

queuing on the approach. Therefore, increasing the follow-up time would decrease the approach capacity. The approach capacity is calculated as a mathematical function of critical gap and follow up time, as well as conflicting circulating traffic volumes. The limitations of this method are its insensitivity to the roundabout geometric parameters and its applicability to only single-lane roundabouts (Kinzel and Trueblood, 2004).

5.3.1.2 SIDRA
SIDRA as Australian software works with the following four critical parameters to model the roundabout operations (Kinzel and Trueblood, 2004).



Critical Gap (tc): the minimum time interval in the circulating traffic stream that

allows one vehicle to enter the roundabout. A driver would enter the roundabout when faced with any gap equal or greater than the critical gap. Therefore, increasing the critical gap would decrease approach capacity;  Follow-Up Headway (tf): the time between the entries of two vehicles into the

roundabout using the same gap in circulating traffic under a condition of continuous queuing on the approach. Therefore, increasing the follow-up time would decrease the approach capacity;  Intra-Bunch Headway: "Average headway between vehicles in a moving queue

(minimum headway in a traffic stream). This is used in order to define moving queues (bunches) of vehicles for the purpose of modeling headway distribution of vehicles" (Akcelik, 2004) ;

36



O-D Factor: A factor to improve the prediction of capacities under medium to

heavy circulating traffic flow (Akcelik, 2005).

5.3.2 Empirical Models 5.3.2.1 ARCADY
ARCADY (Assessment of Roundabout Capacity and Delay), Australian software, is developed for predicting capacities, queue lengths, delays, and accident risk at roundabouts. ARCADY is a user friendly tool to aid the traffic engineer in designing new roundabouts, as well as assessing the outcome of modification of the existing designs. ARCADY calculations can be applied to roundabouts with up to seven arms. Consequently, it can easily model the busiest and most complex junctions. The program can even estimate the shape of the peak according to the amount of available data (TRL, 2009).

5.3.2.2 RODEL
RODEL is a software program to develop relationships between the geometric parameters and performance of a roundabout. RODEL stands for Roundabout Delay (NYSDOT, 2000). This software was developed based on the delay and queuing information of 86 roundabouts in the United Kingdom in 1970s. The research on 35 roundabouts was reviewed in U.K. in 1997 and the results confirmed that the original equations worked perfectly and no changes on those equations were required (Ourston, 2009). The methodology of RODEL is based on the empirical model, which is noticeably different from the gap-acceptance models. It links the roundabout approach capacity to the 37

following six geometric parameters, in addition to the circulating conflicting flow (Kinzel and Trueblood, 2004).  Entry Width;  Half Width;  Effective Flare Length;  Entry Radius;  Entry Angle;  Inscribed Circle Diameter. These parameters are shown in figure 6.1.

After gathering and reviewing the data of the site, intersecting roadways, and the traffic volumes, RODEL performs a geometric analysis of the proposed roundabout. The outcome of this analysis is the required geometry for accurate function of that roundabout within the desired capacity. It also determines if the geometry of an existing roundabout works appropriately in terms of capacity and delay (Ritchie, 2006). RODEL has been utilized for the study implemented through this thesis regarding the Sensitivity of the geometric parameters in terms of delay. More information about the methodology and details of the RODEL are presented in Chapter 6.

38

CHAPTER SIX: DESIGN PRINCIPLES 6.1 RODEL Software
RODEL (ROundabout DELay) is software for design of roundabouts, developed by Barry Crown of the Staffordshire County Council in England (Ourston, 2009). Improving the quality of design, reducing the design time, reducing land and service costs, and deriving the best layout within the major constraints including cost, delay, and safety are the main purposes of this program. The great characteristic of this software is that it generates geometry of a roundabout before preparation of any drawing. Consequently, it saves a lot of time required for design of a roundabout because checking the amount and relationship of the parameters are through the software instead of repeated drawings (Crown, 2002). RODEL operates in two modes called Mode 1 and Mode 2. Sets of entry geometry including value of queue, delay, RFC (Ratio of Flow to Capacity) are generated in Mode 1 to define the best fit for each approach. Mode 2 generates the concurrent display of input and output in order to repeatedly modify and redefine the selected geometry. "Displaying queues and delays in every 1 or 2 seconds, enables the designer to develop a "feel" for the design to converge an optimum layout within the constraints" (Crown, 2002). RODEL, as a geometric analysis tool to design a roundabout, is utilized after obtaining and reviewing the information regarding roadways (approaches), site conditions, and traffic volumes. The application of RODEL to design a roundabout, with various alternatives to

achieve the best design, is based on the initial lane geometry and the required capacity to handle the measured traffic volume. The calculations are implemented by using empirical equations which are observed and checked with the field data (Ritchie, 2007). 39

6.2 Roundabout Geometric Parameters
There are six key geometric parameters with most influence on the capacity, delay, traffic speed, and safety at a roundabout. These parameters are: Half Width, Entry Width, Effective Flare Length, Entry Radius, Inscribed Circle Diameter, and Entry Angle. Figure 6.1 illustrates the RODEL parameters.

Figure 6.1 Key Parameters of a Roundabout (WisDOT, 2008)

The definition and impact of each geometric parameter on design of a roundabout are discussed in Chapter 8.

40

6.3 Confidence Level
Confidence Level (CL) describes the level of certainty on accuracy of the delays and queues. These parameters are calculated based on flow/volume (V) and capacity (C). There may be error on measurement of the flow and capacity, which would affect on the accuracy of the delays and queues. The error on both capacity and flow is taken into account and is called Confidence Level in RODEL (Lenters , 2003). The standard confidence level used in the industry is 50%. However, the 85th percentile confidence level is used by the designers to check the robustness of a design in terms of capacity and come up with a practical design (WisDOT, 2008). Confidence level 85 is defined by the point representing 85% of Ratio of Flow to Capacity (RFC) which is laid on the steep part of the following curve (Figure 6.2). As it is shown, this part of the curve is the most sensitive section to any small changes in capacity or flow (Crown, 2002). Roundabout Design with confidence level of 85 causes avoiding a gross underestimation of delay (NYSDOT, 2000).

Figure 6.2 Confidence Level illustration (Crown, 2002)

41

The 50th percentile confidence level is the usual confidence level used in all the programs that analyze traffic volumes in terms of operations and level of service. This confidence level (50%) is a default value in RODEL as well. The confidence level used in RODEL can be adjustable to check whether the proposed design will be adequate under recommended geometric parameters. In RODEL, to get the certainty of having adequate roundabout capacity during the peak hours, there is an option called "design check" which adjusts the confidence level from 50th to 85th or even 95th percentile (Ritchie, 2007).

6.4 Level of Service
Level of Service (LOS) is a term to describe the operating conditions of an intersection or a roundabout. Verification of LOS in RODEL is derived from the average delay, in seconds, for each approach of a roundabout. Level of Service varies from LOS "A" to LOS "F". LOS "A" represents complete and flexible operation (within 10 seconds delay) for a driver, whereas, LOS "F" reflects very long delay in operation of a roundabout. It is considered as failed performance (Ritchie, 2007). Table 6.1 shows the level of service criteria for roundabouts.

Table 6.1 Level of Service Criteria at Roundabouts (Whitlock, 2007) ROUNDABOUT LEVEL OF SERVICE A B C D E F AVERAGE CONTROL DELAY (SEC/VEH) DELAY OF 0-10 SECONDS DELAY OF 10-15 SECONDS DELAY OF 15-25 SECONDS DELAY OF 25-35 SECONDS DELAY OF 35-50 SECONDS DELAY OF MORE THAN 50 SECONDS

42

An acceptable LOS depends on the context of the roundabout. In busy downtown areas LOS "E" could be acceptable, while it may be unacceptable at rural intersections with low traffic volume. According to many articles, LOS "D" with the average delay of 25 to 35 seconds is considered as an acceptable LOS (WisDOT, 2008), (Ritchie, 2007), (Whitlock, 2007), (Lakkundi, et. al., 2004), and (Stanek and Milam, 2004). In this study, LOS "C" with the average delay of 15 to 25 seconds is the limit for proper operation of the studied roundabouts.

43

CHAPTER SEVEN: PARAMETER PROCESSING STRATEGIES
The methodology of this study contains two parts. The first part was inputting the data of three existing roundabouts into the RODEL, monitoring the obtained delays and LOS in excel sheets, and creating six tables of results for each roundabout (18 tables in total) which are depicted in the Appendix A. Then, different sets of graphs were produced to analyze the results of this study and find the sensitivity of each parameter. The second part was inputting the records of the created tables into the MiniTab program. MiniTab is a statistical program that improves the clarity of the products by analyzing the data. This program has been used to verify the data analysis. It also confirms the attained sensitivities of the graphs and calculates the mean value of each parameter in order to determine the accuracy of each precision. The mean value of the average delay related to each parameter represents the best performance of that parameter. Appendix "B" includes tables showing the mean value of the average delay times for each roundabout with different AADTs, time-frames, and confidence levels. These values correspond to a range of each geometric parameter amount which is a percentage of its original data (with 5% intervals). This is the check range that designer should consider to verify the optimum amount of each parameter. The precision of each parameter is determined through the closeness of the amount of that parameter, based on the mean average delay, to the original data. It has been calculated as: 100 ­ check range in Appendix B. The level of accuracy for each parameter is discussed in chapter eight.

44

7.1 Database
The delay investigation took place on three existing roundabouts in the Region of Waterloo. The required data for this purpose consists of the traffic volume of each roundabout in three time-frames including 8.30-9.30 AM, 12.30-1.30 PM, and 4.30-5.30 PM (peak hours). In addition, their geometric information including entry width, flare length, half width, entry radius, entry angle (phi), and inscribed circle diameter are required. These geometric features vary based on the number of roundabouts' approaches and the number of lanes on each approach.

7.2 Analysis
One of the significant advantages of using roundabout rather than traditional intersections is delay reduction. This reduction is the outcome of having yield control signs instead of stop signs or traffic signals. Furthermore, the flare of a roundabout causes increasing the traffic capacity and decreasing the delay. As explained in the previous chapters, there are six geometric parameters involved in designing a roundabout by RODEL. In order to have a precise design and minimize the delay, knowing the sensitivity and accuracy of each geometric parameter is vital. The sensitivity and precision of the roundabout geometric parameters are analyzed through the following stages: 1. The available geometric data of the above noted three roundabouts in the Region of Waterloo have been taken into account as the original data. A deviation of up to ± 50%, with 5% intervals, has been considered to determine the sensitivity and precision of each parameter through the RODEL. However, the range of variation was limited to the practical range of each parameter. Then, the results are graphed using Excel. The graphs reflect the affiliation between the delay and each parameter. The practical range is illustrated in Table 7.1.

49

Table 7.1 The practical range for geometric parameters of roundabout (Robinson, et al., 1993) PARAMETER ENTRY WIDTH HALF WIDTH FLARE LENGTH ENTRY RADIUS ENTRY ANGLE INSCRIBED CIRCL DIAMETER PRACTICAL RANGE 4-15 meters 2-7.3 meters 1-100 meters 6-100 meters 10-60 degrees 15-100 meters

2. The available Annual Average Daily Traffic (AADT) of each roundabout has been taken into account as the original traffic data for the peak hours of each time frame. It is obvious that more traffic volume causes more delay if the capacity of an intersection or a roundabout is insufficient. This stage of analysis is to find out the impacts of different traffic volumes on the delay. In this respect, four sets of traffic volumes have been considered to monitor the related delays. The first set of the traffic volume was the original traffic data of the roundabouts at the Region of Waterloo. The second, third, and fourth sets have been set at AADT of 2000, 3000, and 4000 vehicles respectively. This stage reflects the association between the different traffic volumes and each geometric parameter to find the amount of delay through RODEL. The above noted stages have been completed with two confidence levels of 50 and 85 to cover both the industry standard and the most sensitive confidence level, as it is mentioned in Chapter 6.

50

CHAPTER EIGHT: TEST RESULTS AND ANALYSIS
There is a reverse relationship between traffic capacity and delay at any intersection (the more capacity provided, the less traffic delay occurs). Delay ignorance may cause wrong/impossible predicting of the travel time and lead to an inaccurate model output. Therefore, delay minimization is one of the most essential goals of transportation planning applications to improve the performance of planning models (Ding, 2007).

8.1 Parameters Analysis
The following sections reflect the analysis outcomes of the parameters which are prioritized based on their sensitivities. The roundabout design parameters, prioritized based on their sensitivities, are: Half Width, Entry Width, Flare Length, Entry Radius, Entry Angle (Phi), and Inscribed Circle Diameter. For the analysis purpose, two sets of graphs have been created for each roundabout. Each graph includes different curves that represent the sensitivity of each parameter within the morning, noon, and afternoon peak hours. The conclusions of this study have come from the comparison of those curves shown in the final graphs of this chapter.

8.1.1 Half Width
Half Width, which is defined as the width of approach roadway is one of the most sensitive parameters and has the most impact on the capacity and delay on a roundabout. Figures 8.1 and 8.2 illustrate the sensitivity of this parameter in different traffic volumes and timeframes. Every graph includes different curves and each curve reflects the relationship between the traffic volume and the average delay. These graphs consist of more than 109 and 82 data 51

points for the studied roundabouts with four and three approaches, respectively. Each data point is the Half Width parameter of every approach of the roundabouts on different deviations from the original data within the practical range.

Figure 8.1 Half Width for all three roundabouts with confidence level 50

Figure 8.2 Half Width for all three roundabouts with confidence level 85

52

As it is shown on the graphs the Half Width parameter does not have any adverse impact on the roundabouts operation with the original traffic volume and the AADT of 2000 vehicles. The LOS is "A" which means having less than 10 seconds of delay. The sensitivity of this parameter changes significantly by increasing the traffic volume from 2000 to 3000 vehicles and more, especially with the confidence level of 85. An example of this situation is on Erb/Erbsville/Ira-Needle roundabout that at AADT of 3000 vehicles and confidence level of 50, the occurred LOS is "C", "B", and "A" for the morning and noon peak hours; and "B" and "A" for the afternoon peak hours. Therefore, the LOS "C" causes the curve changes to become noticeable in the graph for the morning and noon hours. The LOS at the AADT of 3000 vehicles and confidence level of 85 varies between "F" to "A" for different timeframes. The occurred LOS at the same roundabout with the AADT of 4000 vehicles and confidence level of 85 are "F" for all the cases. The LOS changes, which represent the sensitivity of this parameter, are shown in Tables 8.1, 8.2 and 8.3 for the studied roundabouts. 8.1.3 Flare Length
Flare Length is defined as the transition distance between the point of half a width and the yield line. It is the third sensitive parameter in determining the average delay. Flare length and Entry Width parameters are related. The longer the Flare length is, the shorter delay occurs. The shorter delay cause increasing the capacity of the roundabout and higher speed of the entry vehicles. This higher speed would decrease the deflection of the roundabout. Figures 8.5 and 8.6 illustrate the sensitivity of this parameter in different traffic volumes and time-frames. Every graph includes different curves and each curve reflects the relationship between the traffic volume and the average delay. These graphs consist of more than 251 and 172 data points for the roundabouts with four and three approaches, respectively. Each data point is the Flare Length

59

parameter of every approach of the studied roundabouts on different deviations from the original data within the practical range.

Figure 8.5 Flare Length for all three roundabouts with confidence level 50

Figure 8.6 Flare Length for all three roundabouts with confidence level 85

As it is shown on the graphs, the Flare Length parameter does not have any adverse impact on the operation of the roundabouts with the original traffic volume. The LOS is "A" 60

which means having less than 10 seconds of delay. However, the LOS varies from "A" to "B", "C", "D", "E" and even "F" with increasing the AADT on confidence levels of 50 and 85. An example of this situation is on Congestoga/Can-Amera roundabout that at AADT of 2000 vehicles and confidence level of 85, the occurred LOS is "C", "B", and "A" for the morning peak hours, "A" for the noon peak hours, and "B" and "A" for the afternoon peak hours. Therefore, the LOS "C" of the morning time-frame causes the curve changes become noticeable in the graph. The occurred LOS at the same roundabout with the AADT of 3000 vehicles and confidence levels of 50 and 85 are "F" for all the cases. The LOS changes for the studied roundabouts are shown in the Tables 8.7, 8.8, and 8.9.

8.1.5 Entry Angle (Phi)
Entry Angle (Phi Angle) is defined as the angle between tangents of the direction of entry into the roundabout and the direction of the nearest exit. Its sensitivity is similar to the Entry Radius's (not very sensitive). In general, the Phi Angle has a linear relationship with the delay and the larger the Phi Angle is, the more delay occurs. Figures 8.9 and 8.10 illustrate the sensitivity of this parameter in different traffic volumes and time-frames. Every graph includes different curves and each curve reflects the relationship between the traffic volume and the average delay. These graphs consist of more than 163, 145, and 172 data points for the roundabouts with four and three approaches, respectively. 8.1.6 Inscribed Circle Diameter
Inscribed Circle Diameter (ICD) is defined as the diameter of the outside curb of the circulating roadway. It is a low sensitive parameter in terms of delay because the changes in the ICD do not significantly change the delay time. However, the ICD is an important parameter and represents as a scale factor to determine the size of a roundabout. Figures 8.11 and 8.12 illustrate the sensitivity of this parameter in different traffic volumes and time-frames. Every graph includes different curves and each curve reflects the relationship between the traffic volume and the average delay. These graphs consist of more than 251 and 172 data points for the roundabouts with four and three approaches, respectively. Each data point is the ICD parameter of every approach of the studied roundabouts on different deviations from the original data within the practical range.

71

"A" to "B", "C", "D", "E" and even "F" with increasing the AADT to 3000 and 4000 vehicles and with confidence levels of 50 and 85. An example of this situation is on Erb/Erbsville/IraNeedle roundabout that at AADT of 3000 vehicles and confidence level of 85, the occurred LOS is "B" and mainly "A". Therefore, the curve changes are not noticeable in the graph. The LOS at the same roundabout with the AADT of 4000 vehicles and confidence level of 50 are " F", "E", "D", and "C" for the morning and afternoon peak hours; and "E", "D", "C", and "B" for the noon peak hours. 8.2 AADT Threshold
As it was mentioned earlier, the analysis for this study has been done in four sets of AADTs: the existing AADT, and the AADT of 2000, 3000, and 4000 vehicles. However, there are more analysis is required to verify the maximum AADT in order to have desirable LOS. Since an acceptable LOS for this thesis is considered LOS "C", the maximum AADT for the studied roundabouts are as follows:  The maximum AADT for the Erb/Erbsville/Ira-Needle Roundabout is 3400 vehicles. Therefore, the average delay becomes 20.3 seconds and the LOS for signalized and un-signalized intersection occur "C".

75



The maximum AADT for the Ira-Needle/Trussler/Highview Roundabout is 3150 vehicles. Therefore, the average delay becomes 24.4 seconds and the LOS for signalized and un-signalized intersection occur "C".



The maximum AADT for the Congestoga/Can-Amera Roundabout is 2200 vehicles. Therefore, the average delay becomes 24.8 seconds and the LOS for signalized and un-signalized intersection occur "C".

76

CHAPTER NINE: CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 9.1 Conclusions
Transportation is an unavoidable application of humans' daily life. One of the main strategies of every country is development of their transportation system because it has a major impact on their economical and social growth. This system facilitates people travelling and merchandise dispatching through the airborne, marine, and overland networks. The main feature of overland transportation system is roads network, which includes the roadways within the urban and rural areas. Intersections are imperative points of a roads network where different roadways meet. A desirable intersection is the one that allows traffic movement with the maximum safety and capacity, as well as the minimum delay. Signalized and un-signalized intersections have been utilized in many countries since twentieth century. Delay and traffic congestion have been the main problems associated with the use of intersections. In the past decade, modern roundabouts have been brought into attention of Canada and the United States as one of the best remedies to overcome the intersection problems and improve their operational performance. Since maximum traffic capacity and minimum delay represent the greatest operational performance of a roundabout, careful data gathering and smart design of the geometric parameters are the key points to have an efficient roundabout. The aim of this thesis is to present the most sensitive geometric parameters of roundabouts in terms of delay. It has also been intended to determine the required precision for each parameter. In this study, numerous data sets of three existing roundabouts in the Region of Waterloo have been analyzed for each geometric parameter within the context of different traffic 77

volumes and time-frames in two confidence levels of 50 and 85. The delay distribution graphs confirmed the level of sensitivity of each parameter. Furthermore, the accuracy of each parameter was obtained through the mean value of delay graphs for every set of traffic volume, time-frame, and confidence level. This precision (mean value) represents the best domain for the amount of each geometric parameter in terms of having the best performance within a certain range of delay. As a result of this study, there are different levels of sensitivity for the six geometric parameters, half width, entry width, flare length, entry radius, entry angle, and inscribed circle diameter in terms of delay. The more sensitive parameters are the ones involved with queuing and more delay at each approach. Those are the entry geometric features including half width, entry width, and flare length. Once vehicles enter into the circulating traffic, the sensitivity of geometric parameters is getting smaller because less delay may occur. Therefore, entry radius, entry angle, and inscribed circle diameter are the less sensitive parameters. The range of accuracy varies from 25% for the most sensitive parameter (half width) to 2.5% for the least sensitive parameter (inscribed circle diameter). The roundabout parameters have different types of relationship with the delay. The wider entry angle (phi angle) causes increasing the delay on a linear basis. The other geometric parameters have a reverse non-linear relationship with the delay. The lower amounts of each parameter results in more delay time.

9.2 Recommendations
Roundabout operation is successful when it responds to the required level of capacity and safety. Therefore, evaluation of the existing roundabouts must be a crucial concern. It is strongly

78

recommended measuring their delay time and queue length during the peak hours of different days and comparing the results with the acceptable delay that has been expected through the design process. If the evaluation of the existing roundabouts reflect significant unexpected delays, reviewing/revising the design criteria for future roundabouts would be necessary. In case of old roundabouts, commonly called old traffic circles, modification of their geometric parameters and converting them to modern roundabouts through a reasonable budget and construction schedule is recommended. Development of the roundabouts is a key accomplishment in increasing the traffic capacity and decreasing the delay at intersections. This can be achieved through a comprehensive program including design, construction, operation, maintenance, and evaluation of new roundabouts. It is a wise idea to consider a pilot project within the context of this program. It is recommended constructing a few roundabouts in every town, region, or sub-division for the purpose of checking their actual performance/efficiency and demonstration of their advantages to residents/drivers. Complete development of the roundabouts can be implemented upon successful operation of the above-noted pilot projects. The driver behaviour while driving on the roundabout approaches and circulating traffic is an important part of an appropriate operational performance. It is recommended to educate drivers parallel with implementation of the roundabouts development program. This can be achieved through various means such as introducing the roundabouts and their advantages in the media, lecturing in the community centers and any other social gatherings, distribution of informative flyers and brochures, and asking more questions in the driving licence tests.

79

There is a logical relationship between traffic delay and air pollution. Less delay causes less vehicle emissions. Since control of the pollution sources is a worldwide concern, further studies regarding environmental aspects of the modern roundabouts is recommended.

80
